## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: materials science / constitutive modeling
**Analysis**: This task describes a stress-strain relationship at different temperatures, which is a classic problem in materials science for modeling the plastic flow and thermal softening behavior of metals and alloys. The target sigma depends on strain epsilon and temperature T, often through power-law hardening and exponential or power-law thermal softening.

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Hollomon power-law hardening: sigma = c0 * epsilon**c1 (isothermal strain hardening without temperature effect)
- Exponential thermal softening: sigma = c0 * exp(c1 * T) (pure temperature effect on stress when strain is constant)
- Power-law with additive temperature: sigma = c0 * epsilon**c1 + c2 * T (strain hardening with linear thermal offset)
- Multiplicative thermal softening of a power-law: sigma = c0 * epsilon**c1 * exp(c2 * T) (combined strain hardening and exponential thermal softening)
- Temperature-dependent strain hardening exponent: sigma = c0 * epsilon**(c1 + c2 * T) (coupling between temperature and strain hardening rate)
- Thermal effect on yield stress plus hardening: sigma = c0 * exp(c1 * T) + c2 * epsilon**c3 (temperature-dependent initial yield and power-law hardening)
- Voce saturation hardening with temperature: sigma = c0 + c1 * (1 - exp(c2 * epsilon)) + c3 * T (saturation of strain hardening at large strain plus thermal shift)
- Multiplicative linear thermal softening: sigma = c0 * epsilon**c1 * (1 + c2 * T) (strain hardening scaled by a linear thermal softening factor)
- Product of power and exponential strain with thermal softening: sigma = c0 * epsilon**c1 * exp(c2 * epsilon) * exp(c3 * T) (power-law hardening with exponential saturation and thermal softening)
- Quadratic strain hardening with temperature: sigma = c0 + c1 * epsilon + c2 * epsilon**2 + c3 * T (second-order polynomial hardening and linear temperature dependence)
- Scaled hyperbolic sine for creep-like flow: sigma = c0 * asinh(c1 * epsilon) * exp(c2 * T) (sine-hyperbolic law for rate-independent approximation of creep with thermal activation)
- Inverse strain saturation: sigma = c0 / (c1 + epsilon) + c2 * T (stress decreasing with strain, e.g., failure or damage modeling, plus temperature)
- Ludwik-type hardening with temperature: sigma = c0 + c1 * epsilon**c2 + c3 * T (yield stress plus power hardening and additive temperature)
- Temperature-modulated exponential hardening: sigma = c0 * exp(c1 * epsilon) * (1 + c2 * T) (exponential hardening with linear thermal softening factor)
- Piecewise linear-plastic with temperature: sigma = c0 * epsilon + c1 * (epsilon - c2) * (epsilon > c2) + c3 * T (bilinear hardening with threshold and temperature shift)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Monotonic increase of stress with strain indicates strain hardening, often captured by power-law (epsilon**c) or exponential (exp(c*epsilon)) forms, possibly with an additive yield stress term.
- Decrease of stress with increasing temperature signals thermal softening; this is commonly modeled by a multiplicative exponential factor exp(c*T) (c<0) or a factor (1 - c*T) for limited temperature ranges.
- Multiplicative coupling f(epsilon)*g(T) is the dominant structure when temperature softens the entire stress-strain curve proportionally without changing the hardening exponent.
- When the strain hardening exponent appears to vary with temperature, this suggests a coupling term like epsilon**(c1 + c2*T) or a product of a power and an exponential of epsilon times a temperature function.
- Saturation of stress at large strains (leveling off) indicates the need for a saturating term like (1 - exp(c*epsilon)) or epsilon/(c + epsilon), often combined with a temperature-dependent saturated value.
- A distinct yield point followed by plastic flow can be modeled using an offset strain (epsilon - c0)_+ or a sigmoidal activation, with temperature possibly shifting the yield point linearly or exponentially.
- If thermal softening is more pronounced at higher strains, this suggests an interaction term like epsilon * T or epsilon**c1 * T, which is mathematically a multiplicative product of temperature and a strain function.
- Linear dependence of stress on temperature (T appearing additively or as a factor (1 + c*T)) is appropriate for modest temperature ranges where no activation-dominated creep occurs.
- When stress-strain curves at different temperatures appear as vertically shifted replicas, additive temperature terms like + c*T work; when they appear as scaled replicas, multiplicative forms f(epsilon) * g(T) are better.
- In metals at high homologous temperatures, an Arrhenius-like factor exp(c/T) often appears, but when restricted to a narrow temperature interval a simpler exponential exp(c*T) or linear term may approximate the softening adequately.

The Ground Truth (GT) expression is:

\[ \text{GT} = c_0 \epsilon \bigl(-c_1(T - c_2) + c_3\bigr) - c_4(T - c_5) + c_6(T - c_7) \log(\epsilon + c_8) \]

\[
\begin{aligned}
\text{GT} &= -c_0c_1 \epsilon(T - C) + c_0\epsilon - c_4(T - C) + c_6(T - C)\log(\epsilon + 1) \\
&= -c_0c_1 \epsilon T + (c_0c_1 C + c_0)\epsilon - c_4 T + c_4 C + c_6 T \log(\epsilon + 1) - c_6 C \log(\epsilon + 1).

\]

The required basis functions are: constant, \(\epsilon\), \(T\), \(\epsilon T\), \(\log(\epsilon+1)\), \(T\log(\epsilon+1)\). No other functions (e.g., \(\epsilon^2\), \(\exp(\cdot)\), \(\sin(\cdot)\), rational denominators in \(T\) or \(\epsilon\)) may appear unless they can be exactly removed by setting constants to zero.

**Analysis of Candidate 30:**

\[ \text{Candidate 30} = \frac{T c_0}{T c_1 + c_2} + \frac{T c_3 \epsilon}{T c_1 + c_2} + \frac{T c_4 \log(c_5 \epsilon + 1)}{T c_1 + c_2} + \frac{c_6 \epsilon}{T c_1 + c_2} + \frac{c_7 \log(c_5 \epsilon + 1)}{T c_1 + c_2} + \frac{c_8}{T c_1 + c_2}.
\]

Set \(c_1 = 0\) (allowed; constants are free). The denominator becomes the constant \(c_2\). Also set \(c_5 = 1\) to match the log argument \(\epsilon+1\). The expression simplifies to:

\[

\]

This provides exactly the six basis functions present in GT with independent coefficients:

- Coefficient of \(T\): \(c_0/c_2\)
- Coefficient of \(\epsilon T\): \(c_3/c_2\)
- Coefficient of \(T\log(\epsilon+1)\): \(c_4/c_2\)
- Coefficient of \(\epsilon\): \(c_6/c_2\)
- Coefficient of \(\log(\epsilon+1)\): \(c_7/c_2\)
- Constant term: \(c_8/c_2\)
